</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Radioactive waste</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="floatright"><a href="/wiki/File:TINT_Radioactive_wastes%27_barrel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="TINT Radioactive wastes' barrel.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/TINT_Radioactive_wastes%27_barrel.jpg/220px-TINT_Radioactive_wastes%27_barrel.jpg" width="220" height="293" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/TINT_Radioactive_wastes%27_barrel.jpg/330px-TINT_Radioactive_wastes%27_barrel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/TINT_Radioactive_wastes%27_barrel.jpg/440px-TINT_Radioactive_wastes%27_barrel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a></div>
<p><b>Radioactive waste</b> is <a href="/wiki/Waste" title="Waste">waste</a> that contains <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> material. Radioactive waste is usually a <a href="/wiki/By-product" title="By-product">by-product</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> generation and other applications of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_technology" title="Nuclear technology">nuclear technology</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_physics" title="Nuclear physics">research</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">medicine</a>. Radioactive waste is <a href="/wiki/Radiobiology" title="Radiobiology">hazardous</a> to most forms of life and the environment, and is <a href="/wiki/Regulation" title="Regulation">regulated</a> by government agencies in order to protect human health and the environment.</p>
<p>Radioactivity naturally decays over time, so radioactive waste has to be isolated and confined in appropriate disposal facilities for a sufficient period until it no longer poses a threat. The time radioactive waste must be stored for depends on the type of waste and radioactive isotopes. Current major approaches to managing radioactive waste have been segregation and storage for short-lived waste, near-surface disposal for low and some intermediate level waste, and <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">deep burial</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">partitioning / transmutation</a> for the high-level waste.</p>
<p>A summary of the amounts of radioactive waste and management approaches for most developed countries are presented and reviewed periodically as part of the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a> (IAEA) <a href="/wiki/Joint_Convention_on_the_Safety_of_Spent_Fuel_Management_and_on_the_Safety_of_Radioactive_Waste_Management" title="Joint Convention on the Safety of Spent Fuel Management and on the Safety of Radioactive Waste Management">Joint Convention on the Safety of Spent Fuel Management and on the Safety of Radioactive Waste Management</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_and_significance_of_radioactive_waste">Nature and significance of radioactive waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nature and significance of radioactive waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Radioactive waste typically comprises a number of <a href="/wiki/Radionuclides" class="mw-redirect" title="Radionuclides">radionuclides</a>: unstable configurations of elements that <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">decay</a>, emitting <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a> which can be harmful to humans and the environment. Those isotopes emit different types and levels of radiation, which last for different periods of time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fission_product_yield" title="Fission product yield">Fission product yield</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">Radioactive decay</a></div>

<td colspan="5" align="center">Hover <span title="and get popups like this" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">underlined</span>: more info</td>

</table>
<p>The radioactivity of all radioactive waste diminishes with time. All radionuclides contained in the waste have a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a>—the time it takes for half of the atoms to decay into another <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a>—and eventually all radioactive waste decays into non-radioactive elements (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Stable_nuclide" title="Stable nuclide">stable nuclides</a>). Certain radioactive elements (such as <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>) will remain hazardous to humans and other creatures for hundreds or thousands of years. Other radionuclides remain radioactive for millions of years (though most of these products have so little activity as a result of their long half-lives that their radiation is lost in the <a href="/wiki/Background_radiation" title="Background radiation">background level</a>). Thus, these wastes must be shielded for centuries and isolated from the living <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a> for millennia.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Since radioactive decay follows the half-life rule, the rate of decay is inversely proportional to the duration of decay. In other words, the radiation from a long-lived isotope like <a href="/wiki/Iodine-129" title="Iodine-129">iodine-129</a> will be much less intense than that of a short-lived isotope like <a href="/wiki/Iodine-131" title="Iodine-131">iodine-131</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The two tables show some of the major radioisotopes, their half-lives, and their <a href="/wiki/Fission_product_yield" title="Fission product yield">radiation yield</a> as a proportion of the yield of fission of uranium-235.</p>
<p>The energy and the type of the <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a> emitted by a radioactive substance are also important factors in determining its threat to humans.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The chemical properties of the radioactive <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> will determine how mobile the substance is and how likely it is to spread into the environment and <a href="/wiki/Contaminate" class="mw-redirect" title="Contaminate">contaminate</a> humans.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> This is further complicated by the fact that many radioisotopes do not decay immediately to a stable state but rather to radioactive <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">decay products</a> within a <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> before ultimately reaching a stable state.</p>

</table>
<p>Exposure to radioactive waste may cause serious harm or death. In <a href="/wiki/Humans" class="mw-redirect" title="Humans">humans</a>, a dose of 1 <a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">sievert</a> carries a 5.5% risk of developing cancer,<sup id="cite_ref-ICRP103_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICRP103-11">[11]</a></sup> and this risk is assumed to be <a href="/wiki/Linear_no-threshold_model" title="Linear no-threshold model">linearly proportional to dose</a> even for low doses. Ionizing radiation causes deletions in chromosomes.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> If a developing organism such as an <a href="/wiki/Unborn_child" class="mw-redirect" title="Unborn child">unborn child</a> is irradiated, it is possible a <a href="/wiki/Birth_defect" class="mw-redirect" title="Birth defect">birth defect</a> may be induced, but it is unlikely this defect will be in a <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gamete</a> or a gamete-forming <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell</a>. The incidence of radiation-induced mutations in humans is small, as in most mammals, because of natural cellular-repair mechanisms, many just now coming to light. These mechanisms range from DNA, mRNA and protein repair, to internal lysosomic digestion of defective proteins, and even induced cell suicide—apoptosis<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Depending on the decay mode and the <a href="/wiki/Pharmacokinetics" title="Pharmacokinetics">pharmacokinetics</a> <a href="/wiki/Radiopharmacology" title="Radiopharmacology">of an element</a> (how the body processes it and how quickly), the threat due to exposure to a given activity of a <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotope</a> will differ. For instance <a href="/wiki/Iodine-131" title="Iodine-131">iodine-131</a> is a short-lived <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_decay" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma decay">gamma</a> emitter, but because it concentrates in the <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a> gland, it is more able to cause injury than <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a>-137 which, being <a href="/wiki/Water_soluble" class="mw-redirect" title="Water soluble">water soluble</a>, is rapidly excreted in urine. In a similar way, the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha</a> emitting actinides and <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a> are considered very harmful as they tend to have long <a href="/wiki/Biological_half-life" title="Biological half-life">biological half-lives</a> and their radiation has a high <a href="/wiki/Relative_biological_effectiveness" title="Relative biological effectiveness">relative biological effectiveness</a>, making it far more damaging to tissues per amount of energy deposited. Because of such differences, the rules determining biological injury differ widely according to the radioisotope, time of exposure and sometimes also the nature of the chemical compound which contains the radioisotope.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_of_waste">Sources of waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Sources of waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Radioactive waste comes from a number of sources. In countries with nuclear power plants, nuclear armament, or nuclear fuel treatment plants, the majority of waste originates from the nuclear fuel cycle and nuclear weapons reprocessing, otherwise there is no waste of nuclear origin.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Other sources include medical and industrial wastes, as well as naturally occurring radioactive materials (NORM) that can be concentrated as a result of the processing or consumption of coal, oil and gas, and some minerals, as discussed below.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_fuel_cycle">Nuclear fuel cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Nuclear fuel cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">Nuclear fuel cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">Spent nuclear fuel</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">Nuclear power</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Front_end">Front end</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Front end">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Waste from the front end of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel cycle</a> is usually alpha-emitting waste from the extraction of uranium. It often contains radium and its decay products.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">Uranium dioxide</a> (UO<sub>2</sub>) concentrate from mining is not very radioactive&#160;– only a thousand or so times as radioactive as the <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> used in buildings. It is refined from <a href="/wiki/Yellowcake" title="Yellowcake">yellowcake</a> (U<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>), then converted to <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a> gas (UF<sub>6</sub>). As a gas, it undergoes <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enrichment</a> to increase the <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">U-235</a> content from 0.7% to about 4.4% (LEU). It is then turned into a hard <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> oxide (UO<sub>2</sub>) for assembly as reactor fuel elements.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main by-product of enrichment is <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">depleted uranium</a> (DU), principally the <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">U-238</a> isotope, with a U-235 content of ~0.3%. It is stored, either as UF<sub>6</sub> or as U<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>. Some is used in applications where its extremely high density makes it valuable such as <a href="/wiki/Anti-tank" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-tank">anti-tank</a> <a href="/wiki/KE-penetrator" class="mw-redirect" title="KE-penetrator">shells</a>, even sailboat <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keels</a> on at least <a href="/wiki/Pen_Duick" title="Pen Duick">one occasion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> It is also used with plutonium for making <a href="/wiki/Mixed_oxide_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed oxide fuel">mixed oxide fuel</a> (MOX) and to dilute, or <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium#Downblending" title="Enriched uranium">downblend</a>, highly enriched uranium from weapons stockpiles which is now being redirected to become reactor fuel.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Back_end">Back end</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Back end">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a></div>
<p>The back end of the nuclear fuel cycle, mostly spent <a href="/wiki/Fuel_rod" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuel rod">fuel rods</a>, contains <a href="/wiki/Fission_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Fission product">fission products</a> that emit beta and gamma radiation, and <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> that emit <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Uranium-234" title="Uranium-234">uranium-234</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptunium-237" class="mw-redirect" title="Neptunium-237">neptunium-237</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a> and <a href="/wiki/Americium-241" title="Americium-241">americium-241</a>, and even sometimes some neutron emitters such as <a href="/wiki/Californium" title="Californium">californium</a> (Cf). These isotopes are formed in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>.</p>
<p>It is important to distinguish the processing of uranium to make fuel from the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">reprocessing</a> of used fuel. Used fuel contains the highly radioactive products of fission (see high level waste below). Many of these are neutron absorbers, called <a href="/wiki/Neutron_poison" title="Neutron poison">neutron poisons</a> in this context. These eventually build up to a level where they absorb so many neutrons that the chain reaction stops, even with the control rods completely removed. At that point the fuel has to be replaced in the reactor with fresh fuel, even though there is still a substantial quantity of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> present. In the United States, this used fuel is usually "stored", while in other countries such as Russia, the United Kingdom, France, Japan and India, the fuel is reprocessed to remove the fission products, and the fuel can then be re-used, thus cutting costs, reducing health risks, saving time, and in general being far safer.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The fission products removed from the fuel are a concentrated form of high-level waste as are the chemicals used in the process. While these countries reprocess the fuel carrying out single plutonium cycles, India is the only country known to be planning multiple plutonium recycling schemes.<sup id="cite_ref-reprocess_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reprocess-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_composition_and_long_term_radioactivity">Fuel composition and long term radioactivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fuel composition and long term radioactivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">Spent nuclear fuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/High_level_waste" class="mw-redirect" title="High level waste">High level waste</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Activityofuranium233.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Activityofuranium233.jpg/220px-Activityofuranium233.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Activityofuranium233.jpg/330px-Activityofuranium233.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Activityofuranium233.jpg/440px-Activityofuranium233.jpg 2x" data-file-width="570" data-file-height="391" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Activityofuranium233.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Activity of U-233 for three fuel types</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Activitytotal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Activitytotal.jpg/220px-Activitytotal.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Activitytotal.jpg/330px-Activitytotal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Activitytotal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="402" data-file-height="263" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Activitytotal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Total activity for three fuel types</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Long-lived radioactive waste from the back end of the fuel cycle is especially relevant when designing a complete waste management plan for <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a> (SNF). When looking at long term radioactive decay, the actinides in the SNF have a significant influence due to their characteristically long half-lives. Depending on what a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear reactor technology">nuclear reactor</a> is fueled with, the actinide composition in the SNF will be different.</p>
<p>An example of this effect is the use of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuels</a> with <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>. Th-232 is a fertile material that can undergo a neutron capture reaction and two beta minus decays, resulting in the production of fissile <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233">U-233</a>. The SNF of a cycle with thorium will contain U-233. Its radioactive decay will strongly influence the long-term <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">activity</a> curve of the SNF around 1 million years. A comparison of the activity associated to U-233 for three different SNF types can be seen in the figure on the top right.</p>
<p>The burnt fuels are thorium with reactor-grade plutonium (RGPu), thorium with weapons-grade plutonium (WGPu) and <a href="/wiki/MOX_fuel" title="MOX fuel">Mixed Oxide fuel</a> (MOX). For RGPu and WGPu, the initial amount of U-233 and its decay around 1 million years can be seen. This has an effect in the total activity curve of the three fuel types. The absence of U-233 and its daughter products in the MOX fuel results in a lower activity in region 3 of the figure on the bottom right, whereas for RGPu and WGPu the curve is maintained higher due to the presence of U-233 that has not fully decayed.</p>
<p>The use of different fuels in nuclear reactors results in different SNF composition, with varying activity curves.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proliferation_concerns">Proliferation concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Proliferation concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reactor-grade_plutonium" title="Reactor-grade plutonium">Reactor-grade plutonium</a></div>
<p>Since uranium and plutonium are <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a> materials, there have been proliferation concerns. Ordinarily (in <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a>), plutonium is <a href="/wiki/Reactor-grade_plutonium" title="Reactor-grade plutonium">reactor-grade plutonium</a>. In addition to <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>, which is highly suitable for building nuclear weapons, it contains large amounts of undesirable contaminants: <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-240" title="Plutonium-240">plutonium-240</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-241" title="Plutonium-241">plutonium-241</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a>. These isotopes are extremely difficult to separate, and more cost-effective ways of obtaining fissile material exist (e.g. uranium enrichment or dedicated plutonium production reactors).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>High-level waste is full of highly radioactive <a href="/wiki/Fission_products" class="mw-redirect" title="Fission products">fission products</a>, most of which are relatively short-lived. This is a concern since if the waste is stored, perhaps in <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">deep geological storage</a>, over many years the fission products decay, decreasing the radioactivity of the waste and making the plutonium easier to access. The undesirable contaminant Pu-240 decays faster than the Pu-239, and thus the quality of the bomb material increases with time (although its quantity decreases during that time as well). Thus, some have argued, as time passes, these deep storage areas have the potential to become "plutonium mines", from which material for nuclear weapons can be acquired with relatively little difficulty. Critics of the latter idea have pointed out the difficulty of recovering useful material from sealed deep storage areas makes other methods preferable. Specifically, the high radioactivity and heat (80 C in surrounding rock) greatly increases the difficulty of mining a storage area, and the enrichment methods required have high capital costs.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pu-239 decays to <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">U-235</a> which is suitable for weapons and which has a very long half-life (roughly 10<sup>9</sup> years). Thus plutonium may decay and leave uranium-235. However, modern reactors are only moderately enriched with U-235 relative to U-238, so the U-238 continues to serve as a <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(fissile_materials)" title="Denaturation (fissile materials)">denaturation</a> agent for any U-235 produced by plutonium decay.</p>
<p>One solution to this problem is to recycle the plutonium and use it as a fuel e.g. in <a href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fast reactor">fast reactors</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Integral_Fast_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Integral Fast Reactor">pyrometallurgical fast reactors</a>, the separated plutonium and uranium are contaminated by actinides and cannot be used for nuclear weapons.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_weapons_decommissioning">Nuclear weapons decommissioning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nuclear weapons decommissioning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Waste from nuclear weapons decommissioning is unlikely to contain much beta or gamma activity other than <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a>. It is more likely to contain alpha-emitting actinides such as Pu-239 which is a fissile material used in bombs, plus some material with much higher specific activities, such as Pu-238 or Po.</p>
<p>In the past the neutron trigger for an <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">atomic bomb</a> tended to be <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a> and a high activity alpha emitter such as <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a>; an alternative to polonium is <a href="/wiki/Pu-238" class="mw-redirect" title="Pu-238">Pu-238</a>. For reasons of national security, details of the design of modern bombs are normally not released to the open literature.</p>
<p>Some designs might contain a <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generator</a> using Pu-238 to provide a long lasting source of electrical power for the electronics in the device.</p>
<p>It is likely that the fissile material of an old bomb which is due for refitting will contain decay products of the plutonium isotopes used in it, these are likely to include <a href="/wiki/U-236" class="mw-redirect" title="U-236">U-236</a> from Pu-240 impurities, plus some U-235 from decay of the Pu-239; due to the relatively long half-life of these Pu isotopes, these wastes from radioactive decay of bomb core material would be very small, and in any case, far less dangerous (even in terms of simple radioactivity) than the Pu-239 itself.</p>
<p>The beta decay of <a href="/wiki/Pu-241" class="mw-redirect" title="Pu-241">Pu-241</a> forms <a href="/wiki/Am-241" class="mw-redirect" title="Am-241">Am-241</a>; the in-growth of americium is likely to be a greater problem than the decay of Pu-239 and Pu-240 as the americium is a gamma emitter (increasing external-exposure to workers) and is an alpha emitter which can cause the generation of <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a>. The plutonium could be separated from the americium by several different processes; these would include <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing#Non_aqueous_methods" title="Nuclear reprocessing">pyrochemical</a> processes and aqueous/organic <a href="/wiki/Solvent_extraction" class="mw-redirect" title="Solvent extraction">solvent extraction</a>. A truncated <a href="/wiki/PUREX" title="PUREX">PUREX</a> type extraction process would be one possible method of making the separation. Naturally occurring uranium is not fissile because it contains 99.3% of U-238 and only 0.7% of U-235.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_waste">Legacy waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Legacy waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to historic activities typically related to radium industry, uranium mining, and military programs, there are numerous sites that contain or are contaminated with radioactivity. In the United States alone, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">Department of Energy</a> states there are "millions of gallons of radioactive waste" as well as "thousands of tons of <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a> and material" and also "huge quantities of contaminated soil and water."<sup id="cite_ref-usemdoefyp_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usemdoefyp-20">[20]</a></sup> Despite copious quantities of waste, the DOE has stated a goal of cleaning all presently contaminated sites successfully by 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-usemdoefyp_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usemdoefyp-20">[20]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fernald,_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="Fernald, Ohio">Fernald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> site for example had "31 million pounds of uranium product", "2.5 billion pounds of waste", "2.75 million cubic yards of contaminated soil and debris", and a "223 acre portion of the underlying Great Miami Aquifer had uranium levels above drinking standards."<sup id="cite_ref-usemdoefyp_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usemdoefyp-20">[20]</a></sup> The United States has at least 108 sites designated as areas that are contaminated and unusable, sometimes many thousands of acres.<sup id="cite_ref-usemdoefyp_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usemdoefyp-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> DOE wishes to clean or mitigate many or all by 2025, using the recently developed method of <a href="/wiki/Geomelting" title="Geomelting">geomelting</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> however the task can be difficult and it acknowledges that some may never be completely remediated. In just one of these 108 larger designations, <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a>, there were for example at least "167 known contaminant release sites" in one of the three subdivisions of the 37,000-acre (150&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) site.<sup id="cite_ref-usemdoefyp_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usemdoefyp-20">[20]</a></sup> Some of the U.S. sites were smaller in nature, however, cleanup issues were simpler to address, and DOE has successfully completed cleanup, or at least closure, of several sites.<sup id="cite_ref-usemdoefyp_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usemdoefyp-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">Medical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Medical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Radioactive <a href="/wiki/Medical_waste" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical waste">medical waste</a> tends to contain <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a> emitters. It can be divided into two main classes. In diagnostic <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">nuclear medicine</a> a number of short-lived gamma emitters such as <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m" title="Technetium-99m">technetium-99m</a> are used. Many of these can be disposed of by leaving it to decay for a short time before disposal as normal waste. Other isotopes used in medicine, with half-lives in parentheses, include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">Y-90</a>, used for treating <a href="/wiki/Lymphoma" title="Lymphoma">lymphoma</a> (2.7 days)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Radioiodine" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioiodine">I-131</a>, used for <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a> function tests and for treating <a href="/wiki/Thyroid_cancer" title="Thyroid cancer">thyroid cancer</a> (8.0 days)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">Sr-89</a>, used for treating <a href="/wiki/Bone_cancer" class="mw-redirect" title="Bone cancer">bone cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_injection" class="mw-redirect" title="Intravenous injection">intravenous injection</a> (52 days)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">Ir-192</a>, used for <a href="/wiki/Brachytherapy" title="Brachytherapy">brachytherapy</a> (74 days)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">Co-60</a>, used for brachytherapy and external radiotherapy (5.3 years)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">Cs-137</a>, used for brachytherapy, external radiotherapy (30 years)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">Industrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Industrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">Industrial</a> source waste can contain <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neutron_emission" title="Neutron emission">neutron</a> or gamma emitters. Gamma emitters are used in <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">radiography</a> while neutron emitting sources are used in a range of applications, such as <a href="/wiki/Oil_well" title="Oil well">oil well</a> logging.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naturally_occurring_radioactive_material_.28NORM.29">Naturally occurring radioactive material (NORM)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Naturally occurring radioactive material (NORM)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uranium_and_thorium_release_from_coal_combustion.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Uranium_and_thorium_release_from_coal_combustion.gif/220px-Uranium_and_thorium_release_from_coal_combustion.gif" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Uranium_and_thorium_release_from_coal_combustion.gif/330px-Uranium_and_thorium_release_from_coal_combustion.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Uranium_and_thorium_release_from_coal_combustion.gif 2x" data-file-width="340" data-file-height="349" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Uranium_and_thorium_release_from_coal_combustion.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Annual release of <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> <a href="/wiki/Radioisotopes" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotopes">radioisotopes</a> from coal combustion, predicted by <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">ORNL</a> to cumulatively amount to 2.9 million tons over the 1937–2040 period, from the combustion of an estimated 637 billion tons of coal worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-ornl_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ornl-23">[23]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Substances containing natural radioactivity are known as <a href="/wiki/Naturally_occurring_radioactive_material" title="Naturally occurring radioactive material">NORM</a>. After human processing that exposes or concentrates this natural radioactivity (such as mining bringing coal to the surface or burning it to produce concentrated ash), it becomes technologically enhanced naturally occurring radioactive material (TENORM).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> A lot of this waste is <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a>-emitting matter from the decay chains of <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>. The main source of radiation in the human body is <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>-40 (<a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><sup>40</sup>K</a>), typically 17 milligrams in the body at a time and 0.4 milligrams/day intake.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Most rocks, due to their components, have a low level of radioactivity. Usually ranging from 1 <a href="/wiki/Millisievert" class="mw-redirect" title="Millisievert">millisievert</a> (mSv) to 13&#160;mSv annually depending on location, average radiation exposure from natural radioisotopes is 2.0&#160;mSv per person a year worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR-26">[26]</a></sup> This makes up the majority of typical total dosage (with mean annual exposure from other sources amounting to 0.6&#160;mSv from medical tests averaged over the whole populace, 0.4&#160;mSv from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, 0.005&#160;mSv from the legacy of past atmospheric nuclear testing, 0.005&#160;mSv occupational exposure, 0.002&#160;mSv from the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a>, and 0.0002&#160;mSv from the nuclear fuel cycle).<sup id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>TENORM is not regulated as restrictively as nuclear reactor waste, though there are no significant differences in the radiological risks of these materials.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal">Coal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Coal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a> contains a small amount of radioactive uranium, barium, thorium and potassium, but, in the case of pure coal, this is significantly less than the average concentration of those elements in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>. The surrounding strata, if shale or mudstone, often contain slightly more than average and this may also be reflected in the ash content of 'dirty' coals.<sup id="cite_ref-ornl_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ornl-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The more active ash minerals become concentrated in the <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a> precisely because they do not burn well.<sup id="cite_ref-ornl_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ornl-23">[23]</a></sup> The radioactivity of fly ash is about the same as black <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a> and is less than <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> rocks, but is more of a concern because a small amount of the fly ash ends up in the atmosphere where it can be inhaled.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> According to U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Council_on_Radiation_Protection_and_Measurements" title="National Council on Radiation Protection and Measurements">NCRP</a> reports, population exposure from 1000-MWe power plants amounts to 490 <a href="/wiki/Person-rem/year" class="mw-redirect" title="Person-rem/year">person-rem/year</a> for coal power plants, 100 times as great as nuclear power plants (4.8 person-rem/year). (The exposure from the complete nuclear fuel cycle from mining to waste disposal is 136 person-rem/year; the corresponding value for coal use from mining to waste disposal is "probably unknown".)<sup id="cite_ref-ornl_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ornl-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_and_gas">Oil and gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Oil and gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Residues from the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">gas</a> industry often contain radium and its decay products. The sulfate scale from an oil well can be very radium rich, while the water, oil and gas from a well often contain <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>. The radon decays to form solid radioisotopes which form coatings on the inside of pipework. In an oil processing plant the area of the plant where <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> is processed is often one of the more contaminated areas of the plant as radon has a similar boiling point to propane.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_radioactive_waste">Classification of radioactive waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Classification of radioactive waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Classifications of radioactive waste varies by country. The IAEA, which publishes the Radioactive Waste Safety Standards (RADWASS), also plays a significant role.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uranium_tailings">Uranium tailings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Uranium tailings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tailings" title="Uranium tailings">Uranium tailings</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fort-greely-low-level-waste.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Fort-greely-low-level-waste.jpg/200px-Fort-greely-low-level-waste.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Fort-greely-low-level-waste.jpg/300px-Fort-greely-low-level-waste.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Fort-greely-low-level-waste.jpg/400px-Fort-greely-low-level-waste.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="540" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fort-greely-low-level-waste.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Removal of very low-level waste</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Uranium tailings are waste by-product materials left over from the rough processing of <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>-bearing <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a>. They are not significantly radioactive. Mill tailings are sometimes referred to as <b>11(e)2 wastes</b>, from the section of the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Energy_Act_of_1946" title="Atomic Energy Act of 1946">Atomic Energy Act of 1946</a> that defines them. Uranium mill tailings typically also contain <a href="/wiki/Dangerous_goods" title="Dangerous goods">chemically hazardous</a> <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy metal (chemistry)">heavy metal</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>. Vast mounds of uranium mill tailings are left at many old mining sites, especially in <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>.</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Uranium_Mill_Tailings_Remedial_Action" title="Uranium Mill Tailings Remedial Action">Uranium Mill Tailings Remedial Action</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-level_waste">Low-level waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Low-level waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Low_level_waste" class="mw-redirect" title="Low level waste">Low level waste</a></b> (<b>LLW</b>) is generated from hospitals and industry, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel cycle</a>. Low-level wastes include <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>, rags, <a href="/wiki/Tool" title="Tool">tools</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothing</a>, filters, and other materials which contain small amounts of mostly short-lived radioactivity. Materials that originate from any region of an Active Area are commonly designated as LLW as a precautionary measure even if there is only a remote possibility of being contaminated with radioactive materials. Such LLW typically exhibits no higher radioactivity than one would expect from the same material disposed of in a non-active area, such as a normal office block.</p>
<p>Some high-activity LLW requires shielding during handling and transport but most LLW is suitable for shallow land burial. To reduce its volume, it is often compacted or incinerated before disposal. Low-level waste is divided into four classes: <b>class A</b>, <b>class B</b>, <b>class C</b>, and <b>Greater Than Class C</b> (<b>GTCC</b>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intermediate-level_waste">Intermediate-level waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Intermediate-level waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_waste_flask_train_at_Bristol_Temple_Meads_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Nuclear_waste_flask_train_at_Bristol_Temple_Meads_02.jpg/200px-Nuclear_waste_flask_train_at_Bristol_Temple_Meads_02.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Nuclear_waste_flask_train_at_Bristol_Temple_Meads_02.jpg/300px-Nuclear_waste_flask_train_at_Bristol_Temple_Meads_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Nuclear_waste_flask_train_at_Bristol_Temple_Meads_02.jpg/400px-Nuclear_waste_flask_train_at_Bristol_Temple_Meads_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_waste_flask_train_at_Bristol_Temple_Meads_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spent fuel <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_flask" title="Nuclear flask">flasks</a> are transported by railway in the United Kingdom. Each flask is constructed of 14&#160;in (360&#160;mm) thick solid steel and weighs in excess of 50 tons</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Intermediate-level waste</b> (<b>ILW</b>) contains higher amounts of radioactivity and in general require shielding, but not cooling.<sup id="cite_ref-nei-20131126_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nei-20131126-32">[32]</a></sup> Intermediate-level wastes includes <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_sludge" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical sludge">chemical sludge</a> and metal <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> cladding, as well as contaminated materials from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">reactor decommissioning</a>. It may be solidified in <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bitumen" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a> for disposal. As a general rule, short-lived waste (mainly non-fuel materials from reactors) is buried in shallow repositories, while long-lived waste (from fuel and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">fuel reprocessing</a>) is deposited in <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">geological repository</a>. U.S. regulations do not define this category of waste; the term is used in Europe and elsewhere.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High-level_waste">High-level waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: High-level waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/High-level_waste" title="High-level waste">High-level waste</a></b> (<b>HLW</b>) is produced by nuclear reactors. The exact definition of HLW differs internationally. After a nuclear fuel rod serves one fuel cycle and is removed from the core, it is considered HLW.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Fuel rods contain <a href="/wiki/Fission_products" class="mw-redirect" title="Fission products">fission products</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Transuranic element">transuranic elements</a> generated in the <a href="/wiki/Reactor_core" class="mw-redirect" title="Reactor core">reactor core</a>. Spent fuel is highly radioactive and often hot. HLW accounts for over 95 percent of the total radioactivity produced in the process of nuclear <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>. The amount of HLW worldwide is currently increasing by about 12,000 <a href="/wiki/Metric_ton" class="mw-redirect" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> every year, which is the equivalent to about 100 <a href="/wiki/Double-decker_bus" title="Double-decker bus">double-decker buses</a> or a two-story structure with a footprint the size of a <a href="/wiki/Basketball_court" title="Basketball court">basketball court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> A 1000-<a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">MW</a> nuclear power plant produces about 27 tonnes of spent nuclear fuel (unreprocessed) every year.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In 2010, there was very roughly estimated to be stored some 250,000 tons of nuclear HLW,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> that does not include amounts that have escaped into the environment from accidents or tests. Japan estimated to hold 17,000 tons of HLW in storage in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> HLW have been shipped to other countries to be stored or reprocessed, and in some cases, shipped back as active fuel.</p>
<p>The ongoing controversy over <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">high-level radioactive waste disposal</a> is a major constraint on the nuclear power’s global expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-finlay10_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finlay10-38">[38]</a></sup> Most scientists agree<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> that the main proposed long-term solution is deep geological burial, either in a mine or a deep borehole. However, almost six decades after commercial nuclear energy began, no government has succeeded in opening such a repository for civilian high-level nuclear waste,<sup id="cite_ref-finlay10_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finlay10-38">[38]</a></sup> although Finland is in the advanced stage of the construction of such facility, the <a href="/wiki/Onkalo_spent_nuclear_fuel_repository" title="Onkalo spent nuclear fuel repository">Onkalo spent nuclear fuel repository</a>. Reprocessing or recycling spent nuclear fuel options already available or under active development still generate waste and so are not a total solution, but can reduce the sheer quantity of waste, and there are many such active programs worldwide. Deep geological burial remains the only responsible way to deal with high-level nuclear waste.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Morris_Operation" title="Morris Operation">Morris Operation</a> is currently the only de facto high-level radioactive waste storage site in the United States.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transuranic_waste">Transuranic waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Transuranic waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Globe icon." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/48px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" width="48" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/73px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/97px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="290" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this article <b>deal primarily with the United States and do not represent a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the subject</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">You may <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a>, discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Radioactive_waste" title="Talk:Radioactive waste">talk page</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_wizard" title="Wikipedia:Article wizard">create a new article</a>, as appropriate.</span> <small><i>(November 2013)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Transuranic_waste" title="Transuranic waste">Transuranic waste</a></b> (<b>TRUW</b>) as defined by U.S. regulations is, without regard to form or origin, waste that is contaminated with <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha</a>-emitting transuranic <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclides</a> with <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a> greater than 20 years and concentrations greater than 100&#160;<a href="/wiki/Curie" title="Curie">nCi</a>/g (3.7&#160;<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">MBq</a>/kg), excluding high-level waste. Elements that have an atomic number greater than uranium are called transuranic ("beyond uranium"). Because of their long half-lives, TRUW is disposed more cautiously than either low- or intermediate-level waste. In the U.S., it arises mainly from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">weapons</a> production, and consists of clothing, tools, rags, residues, debris and other items contaminated with small amounts of radioactive elements (mainly <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>).</p>
<p>Under U.S. law, transuranic waste is further categorized into "contact-handled" (CH) and "remote-handled" (RH) on the basis of the radiation dose rate measured at the surface of the waste container. CH TRUW has a surface dose rate not greater than 200 <a href="/wiki/Mrem" class="mw-redirect" title="Mrem">mrem</a> per hour (2&#160;mSv/h), whereas RH TRUW has a surface dose rate of 200&#160;mrem/h (2&#160;mSv/h) or greater. CH TRUW does not have the very high radioactivity of high-level waste, nor its high heat generation, but RH TRUW can be highly radioactive, with surface dose rates up to 1,000,000 &#160;mrem/h (10,000&#160;mSv/h). The U.S. currently disposes of TRUW generated from military facilities at the <a href="/wiki/Waste_Isolation_Pilot_Plant" title="Waste Isolation Pilot Plant">Waste Isolation Pilot Plant</a> (WIPP) in a deep salt formation in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention_of_waste">Prevention of waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Prevention of waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A theoretical way to reduce waste accumulation is to phase out current reactors in favour of <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">Generation IV Reactors</a> or <a href="/wiki/Liquid_fluoride_thorium_reactor" title="Liquid fluoride thorium reactor">Liquid Fluoride Thorium Reactors</a>, which output less waste per power generated. <a href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fast reactor">Fast reactors</a> can theoretically consume some existing waste. The UK's <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Decommissioning_Authority" title="Nuclear Decommissioning Authority">Nuclear Decommissioning Authority</a> published a position paper in 2014 on the progress on approaches to the management of separated plutonium, which summarises the conclusions of the work that NDA shared with UK government.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management_of_waste">Management of waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Management of waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_waste_container_2010_nevada.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Nuclear_waste_container_2010_nevada.jpg/220px-Nuclear_waste_container_2010_nevada.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Nuclear_waste_container_2010_nevada.jpg/330px-Nuclear_waste_container_2010_nevada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Nuclear_waste_container_2010_nevada.jpg/440px-Nuclear_waste_container_2010_nevada.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2832" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_waste_container_2010_nevada.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Modern medium to high level transport container for nuclear waste</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">High-level radioactive waste management</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_waste_treatment_technologies" title="List of nuclear waste treatment technologies">List of nuclear waste treatment technologies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_effects_of_nuclear_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental effects of nuclear power">Environmental effects of nuclear power</a></div>
<p>Of particular concern in nuclear waste management are two long-lived fission products, Tc-99 (half-life 220,000 years) and I-129 (half-life 15.7 million years), which dominate spent fuel radioactivity after a few thousand years. The most troublesome transuranic elements in spent fuel are Np-237 (half-life two million years) and Pu-239 (half-life 24,000 years).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Nuclear waste requires sophisticated treatment and management to successfully isolate it from interacting with the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. This usually necessitates treatment, followed by a long-term management strategy involving storage, disposal or transformation of the waste into a non-toxic form.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Governments around the world are considering a range of waste management and disposal options, though there has been limited progress toward long-term waste management solutions.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In second half of 20th century, several methods of disposal of radioactive waste were investigated by nuclear nations,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> which are&#160;:</p>
<ul>
<li>"Long term above ground storage", not implemented.</li>
<li>"Disposal in outer space" (for instance, inside the Sun), not implemented - as it would be currently too expensive.</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Deep_borehole_disposal" title="Deep borehole disposal">Deep borehole disposal</a>", not implemented.</li>
<li>"Rock-melting", not implemented.</li>
<li>"Disposal at subduction zones", not implemented.</li>
<li>"<b><a href="/wiki/Ocean_disposal_of_radioactive_waste" title="Ocean disposal of radioactive waste">Ocean disposal</a></b>", done by the USSR, the United Kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Switzerland, the United States, Belgium, France, The Netherlands, Japan, Sweden, Russia, Germany, Italy and South Korea. (1954–93) This is no longer permitted by international agreements.</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Ocean_floor_disposal" title="Ocean floor disposal">Sub seabed disposal</a>", not implemented, not permitted by international agreements.</li>
<li>"Disposal in ice sheets", rejected in <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Antarctic Treaty">Antarctic Treaty</a></li>
<li>"<b>Direct injection</b>", done by USSR and USA.</li>
</ul>
<p>In the USA, waste management policy completely broke down with the ending of work on the incomplete <a href="/wiki/Yucca_Mountain_Repository" class="mw-redirect" title="Yucca Mountain Repository">Yucca Mountain Repository</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BRC_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRC-48">[48]</a></sup> At present there are 70 nuclear power plant sites where <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Spent fuel">spent fuel</a> is stored. A Blue Ribbon Commission was appointed by President Obama to look into future options for this and future waste. A <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">deep geological repository</a> seems to be favored.<sup id="cite_ref-BRC_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRC-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_treatment_of_waste">Initial treatment of waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Initial treatment of waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vitrification">Vitrification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Vitrification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Long-term storage of radioactive waste requires the stabilization of the waste into a form which will neither react nor degrade for extended periods. It is theorized that one way to do this might be through <a href="/wiki/Vitrification" title="Vitrification">vitrification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Currently at <a href="/wiki/Sellafield" title="Sellafield">Sellafield</a> the high-level waste (<a href="/wiki/PUREX" title="PUREX">PUREX</a> first cycle <a href="/wiki/Raffinate" title="Raffinate">raffinate</a>) is mixed with <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> and then calcined. <a href="/wiki/Calcination" title="Calcination">Calcination</a> involves passing the waste through a heated, rotating tube. The purposes of calcination are to evaporate the water from the waste, and de-nitrate the fission products to assist the stability of the glass produced.<sup id="cite_ref-council_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-council-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 'calcine' generated is fed continuously into an induction heated furnace with fragmented <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The resulting glass is a new substance in which the waste products are bonded into the glass matrix when it solidifies. As a melt, this product is poured into <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a> cylindrical containers ("cylinders") in a batch process. When cooled, the fluid solidifies ("vitrifies") into the glass. After being formed, the glass is highly resistant to water.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>After filling a cylinder, a seal is <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welded</a> onto the cylinder head. The cylinder is then washed. After being inspected for external contamination, the steel cylinder is stored, usually in an underground repository. In this form, the waste products are expected to be immobilized for thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The glass inside a cylinder is usually a black glossy substance. All this work (in the United Kingdom) is done using <a href="/wiki/Hot_cell" title="Hot cell">hot cell</a> systems. Sugar is added to control the <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a> chemistry and to stop the formation of the volatile <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium_tetroxide" title="Ruthenium tetroxide">RuO<sub>4</sub></a> containing <a href="/wiki/Ru-106" class="mw-redirect" title="Ru-106">radioactive ruthenium isotopes</a>. In the West, the glass is normally a <a href="/wiki/Borosilicate_glass" title="Borosilicate glass">borosilicate glass</a> (similar to <a href="/wiki/Pyrex" title="Pyrex">Pyrex</a>), while in the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> bloc it is normal to use a <a href="/wiki/Phosphate_glass" title="Phosphate glass">phosphate glass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The amount of fission products in the glass must be limited because some (<a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a>, the other Pt group metals, and <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a>) tend to form metallic phases which separate from the glass. Bulk vitrification uses electrodes to melt soil and wastes, which are then buried underground.<sup id="cite_ref-Waste_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waste-55">[55]</a></sup> In Germany a vitrification plant is in use; this is treating the waste from a small demonstration reprocessing plant which has since been closed down.<sup id="cite_ref-council_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-council-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ion_exchange">Ion exchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ion exchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>It is common for medium active wastes in the nuclear industry to be treated with <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">ion exchange</a> or other means to concentrate the radioactivity into a small volume. The much less radioactive bulk (after treatment) is often then discharged. For instance, it is possible to use a <a href="/wiki/Ferric" title="Ferric">ferric</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> <a href="/wiki/Flocculation" title="Flocculation">floc</a> to remove radioactive metals from aqueous mixtures.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> After the radioisotopes are absorbed onto the ferric hydroxide, the resulting sludge can be placed in a metal drum before being mixed with cement to form a solid waste form.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In order to get better long-term performance (mechanical stability) from such forms, they may be made from a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a> <a href="/wiki/Slag" title="Slag">slag</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a>, instead of normal <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> (made with Portland cement, gravel and sand).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Synroc">Synroc</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Synroc">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Australian <a href="/wiki/Synroc" title="Synroc">Synroc</a> (synthetic rock) is a more sophisticated way to immobilize such waste, and this process may eventually come into commercial use for civil wastes (it is currently being developed for US military wastes). Synroc was invented by Prof Ted Ringwood (a <a href="/wiki/Geochemist" class="mw-redirect" title="Geochemist">geochemist</a>) at the <a href="/wiki/Australian_National_University" title="Australian National University">Australian National University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The Synroc contains <a href="/wiki/Pyrochlore" title="Pyrochlore">pyrochlore</a> and cryptomelane type minerals. The original form of Synroc (Synroc C) was designed for the liquid high level waste (PUREX raffinate) from a <a href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Light water reactor">light water reactor</a>. The main minerals in this Synroc are hollandite (BaAl<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>6</sub>O<sub>16</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Zirconolite" title="Zirconolite">zirconolite</a> (CaZrTi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Perovskite" title="Perovskite">perovskite</a> (CaTiO<sub>3</sub>). The zirconolite and perovskite are hosts for the <a href="/wiki/Actinides" class="mw-redirect" title="Actinides">actinides</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a> will be fixed in the perovskite. The <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> will be fixed in the hollandite.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_term_management_of_waste">Long term management of waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Long term management of waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants#Waste_disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">Economics of new nuclear power plants §&#160;Waste disposal</a></div>
<p>The time frame in question when dealing with radioactive waste ranges from 10,000 to 1,000,000 years,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> according to studies based on the effect of estimated radiation doses.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Researchers suggest that forecasts of health detriment for such periods should be examined critically.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Practical studies only consider up to 100 years as far as effective planning<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and cost evaluations<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> are concerned. Long term behavior of radioactive wastes remains a subject for ongoing research projects in <a href="/wiki/Geoforecasting" title="Geoforecasting">geoforecasting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Above-ground_disposal">Above-ground disposal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Above-ground disposal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">Dry cask storage</a> typically involves taking waste from a <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a> and sealing it (along with an <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a>) in a <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> cylinder, which is placed in a <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> cylinder which acts as a radiation shield. It is a relatively inexpensive method which can be done at a central facility or adjacent to the source reactor. The waste can be easily retrieved for reprocessing.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geologic_disposal">Geologic disposal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Geologic disposal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WIPP_DoE_2014-05-15_5_15_Image_lrg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/WIPP_DoE_2014-05-15_5_15_Image_lrg.jpg/220px-WIPP_DoE_2014-05-15_5_15_Image_lrg.jpg" width="220" height="391" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/WIPP_DoE_2014-05-15_5_15_Image_lrg.jpg/330px-WIPP_DoE_2014-05-15_5_15_Image_lrg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/WIPP_DoE_2014-05-15_5_15_Image_lrg.jpg/440px-WIPP_DoE_2014-05-15_5_15_Image_lrg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1080" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WIPP_DoE_2014-05-15_5_15_Image_lrg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
On Feb. 14, 2014, at the <a href="/wiki/Waste_Isolation_Pilot_Plant" title="Waste Isolation Pilot Plant">Waste Isolation Pilot Plant</a>, radioactive materials leaked from a damaged storage drum (see photo). Analysis of several accidents, by DOE, have shown lack of a "safety culture" at the facility.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The process of selecting appropriate <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">deep final repositories</a> for high level waste and spent fuel is now under way in several countries with the first expected to be commissioned some time after 2010. The basic concept is to locate a large, stable geologic formation and use mining technology to excavate a tunnel, or large-bore <a href="/wiki/Tunnel_boring_machine" title="Tunnel boring machine">tunnel boring machines</a> (similar to those used to drill the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Tunnel" title="Channel Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</a> from England to France) to drill a shaft 500 metres (1,600&#160;ft) to 1,000 metres (3,300&#160;ft) below the surface where rooms or vaults can be excavated for disposal of high-level radioactive waste. The goal is to permanently isolate nuclear waste from the human environment. Many people remain uncomfortable with the immediate <a href="/wiki/Stewardship_cessation" title="Stewardship cessation">stewardship cessation</a> of this disposal system, suggesting perpetual management and monitoring would be more prudent.</p>
