<p>Because some radioactive species have half-lives longer than one million years, even very low container leakage and radionuclide migration rates must be taken into account.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Moreover, it may require more than one half-life until some nuclear materials lose enough radioactivity to cease being lethal to living things. A 1983 review of the Swedish radioactive waste disposal program by the National Academy of Sciences found that country's estimate of several hundred thousand years—perhaps up to one million years—being necessary for waste isolation "fully justified."<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ocean_floor_disposal" title="Ocean floor disposal">Ocean floor disposal</a> of radioactive waste has been suggested by the finding that deep waters in the North Atlantic Ocean do not present an exchange with shallow waters for about 140 years based on oxygen content data recorded over a period of 25 years.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> They include burial beneath a stable <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plain</a>, burial in a <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> zone that would slowly carry the waste downward into the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">Earth's mantle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> and burial beneath a remote natural or human-made island. While these approaches all have merit and would facilitate an international solution to the problem of disposal of radioactive waste, they would require an <a href="/wiki/Basic_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Basic Law">amendment</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea">Law of the Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Article 1 (Definitions), 7., of the 1996 Protocol to the Convention on the Prevention of Marine Pollution by Dumping of Wastes and Other Matter, (the London Dumping Convention) states:</p>
<dl>
<dd>""Sea" means all marine waters other than the internal waters of States, as well as the seabed and the subsoil thereof; it does not include sub-seabed repositories accessed only from land."</dd>
</dl>
<p>The proposed land-based subductive waste disposal method disposes of nuclear waste in a <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> zone accessed from land and therefore is not prohibited by international agreement. This method has been described as the most viable means of disposing of radioactive waste,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and as the state-of-the-art as of 2001 in nuclear waste disposal technology.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Another approach termed Remix &amp; Return<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> would blend high-level waste with <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">uranium mine</a> and mill tailings down to the level of the original radioactivity of the <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">uranium ore</a>, then replace it in inactive uranium mines. This approach has the merits of providing jobs for miners who would double as disposal staff, and of facilitating a cradle-to-grave cycle for radioactive materials, but would be inappropriate for spent reactor fuel in the absence of reprocessing, due to the presence in it of highly toxic radioactive elements such as plutonium.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deep_borehole_disposal" title="Deep borehole disposal">Deep borehole disposal</a> is the concept of disposing of high-level radioactive waste from nuclear reactors in extremely deep boreholes. Deep borehole disposal seeks to place the waste as much as 5 kilometres (3.1&#160;mi) beneath the surface of the Earth and relies primarily on the immense natural geological barrier to confine the waste safely and permanently so that it should never pose a threat to the environment. The Earth's crust contains 120 trillion tons of thorium and 40 trillion tons of uranium (primarily <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">at relatively trace concentrations of parts per million each</a> adding up over the crust's 3 * 10<sup>19</sup> ton mass), among other natural radioisotopes.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Since the fraction of nuclides decaying per unit of time is inversely proportional to an isotope's half-life, the relative radioactivity of the lesser amount of human-produced radioisotopes (thousands of tons instead of trillions of tons) would diminish once the isotopes with far shorter half-lives than the bulk of natural radioisotopes decayed.</p>
<p>In January 2013, <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a> <a href="/wiki/County_council" title="County council">county council</a> rejected UK central government proposals to start work on an underground storage dump for nuclear waste near to the <a href="/wiki/Lake_District_National_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake District National Park">Lake District National Park</a>. "For any host community, there will be a substantial community benefits package and worth hundreds of millions of pounds" said Ed Davey, Energy Secretary, but nonetheless, the local elected body voted 7–3 against research continuing, after hearing evidence from independent geologists that "the fractured strata of the county was impossible to entrust with such dangerous material and a hazard lasting millennia."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmutation">Transmutation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Transmutation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">Nuclear transmutation</a></div>
<p>There have been proposals for reactors that consume nuclear waste and transmute it to other, less-harmful nuclear waste. In particular, the <a href="/wiki/Integral_Fast_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Integral Fast Reactor">Integral Fast Reactor</a> was a proposed nuclear reactor with a nuclear fuel cycle that produced no transuranic waste and in fact, could consume transuranic waste. It proceeded as far as large-scale tests, but was then canceled by the US Government. Another approach, considered safer but requiring more development, is to dedicate <a href="/wiki/Subcritical_reactor" title="Subcritical reactor">subcritical reactors</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a> of the left-over transuranic elements.</p>
<p>An isotope that is found in nuclear waste and that represents a concern in terms of proliferation is Pu-239. The estimated world total of plutonium in the year 2000 was of 1,645 metric tons, of which 210 metric tons had been separated by reprocessing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The large stock of plutonium is a result of its production inside uranium-fueled reactors and of the reprocessing of weapons-grade plutonium during the weapons program. An option for getting rid of this plutonium is to use it as a fuel in a traditional Light Water Reactor (LWR). Several fuel types with differing plutonium destruction efficiencies are under study.</p>
<p>Transmutation was banned in the US in April 1977 by President Carter due to the danger of plutonium proliferation,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> but President Reagan rescinded the ban in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Due to the economic losses and risks, construction of reprocessing plants during this time did not resume. Due to high energy demand, work on the method has continued in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a>. This has resulted in a practical nuclear research reactor called <a href="/wiki/SCK%E2%80%A2CEN" title="SCK•CEN">Myrrha</a> in which transmutation is possible. Additionally, a new research program called ACTINET has been started in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> to make transmutation possible on a large, industrial scale. According to President Bush's Global Nuclear Energy Partnership (GNEP) of 2007, the US is now actively promoting research on transmutation technologies needed to markedly reduce the problem of nuclear waste treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>There have also been theoretical studies involving the use of <a href="/wiki/Fusion_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fusion reactor">fusion reactors</a> as so called "actinide burners" where a fusion reactor <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> such as in a <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a>, could be "doped" with a small amount of the "minor" transuranic atoms which would be transmuted (meaning fissioned in the actinide case) to lighter elements upon their successive bombardment by the very high energy neutrons produced by the fusion of <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> in the reactor. A study at <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> found that only 2 or 3 fusion reactors with parameters similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/International_Thermonuclear_Experimental_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor">International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor</a> (ITER) could transmute the entire annual <a href="/wiki/Minor_actinide" title="Minor actinide">minor actinide</a> production from all of the <a href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Light water reactor">light water reactors</a> presently operating in the <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors#Power_station_reactors_18" title="List of nuclear reactors">United States fleet</a> while simultaneously generating approximately 1 <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">gigawatt</a> of power from each reactor.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-use_of_waste">Re-use of waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Re-use of waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a></div>
<p>Another option is to find applications for the isotopes in nuclear waste so as to <a href="/wiki/Re-use" class="mw-redirect" title="Re-use">re-use</a> them.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Already, <a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">caesium-137</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strontium-90" title="Strontium-90">strontium-90</a> and a few other isotopes are extracted for certain industrial applications such as <a href="/wiki/Food_irradiation" title="Food irradiation">food irradiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generators" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generators">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a>. While re-use does not eliminate the need to manage radioisotopes, it reduces the quantity of waste produced.</p>
<p>The Nuclear Assisted Hydrocarbon Production Method,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Canadian patent application 2,659,302, is a method for the temporary or permanent storage of nuclear waste materials comprising the placing of waste materials into one or more repositories or boreholes constructed into an <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">unconventional oil</a> formation. The thermal flux of the waste materials fracture the formation and alters the chemical and/or physical properties of hydrocarbon material within the subterranean formation to allow removal of the altered material. A mixture of hydrocarbons, hydrogen, and/or other formation fluids is produced from the formation. The radioactivity of high-level radioactive waste affords proliferation resistance to plutonium placed in the periphery of the repository or the deepest portion of a borehole.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">Breeder reactors</a> can run on U-238 and transuranic elements, which comprise the majority of spent fuel radioactivity in the 1,000–100,000-year time span.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_disposal">Space disposal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Space disposal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Space disposal is attractive because it removes nuclear waste from the planet. It has significant disadvantages, such as the potential for catastrophic failure of a <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a>, which could spread radioactive material into the atmosphere and around the world. A high number of launches would be required because no individual rocket would be able to carry very much of the material relative to the total amount that needs to be disposed of. This makes the proposal impractical economically and it increases the risk of at least one or more launch failures.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> To further complicate matters, international agreements on the regulation of such a program would need to be established.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Costs and inadequate reliability of modern rocket launch systems for space disposal has been one of the motives for interest in <a href="/wiki/Non-rocket_spacelaunch" title="Non-rocket spacelaunch">non-rocket space launch</a> systems such as <a href="/wiki/Mass_driver#On_Earth" title="Mass driver">mass drivers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_elevator" title="Space elevator">space elevators</a>, and other proposals<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_management_plans">National management plans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: National management plans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">High-level radioactive waste management</a></div>
<p>Most countries are considerably ahead of the United States in developing plans for high-level radioactive waste disposal. Sweden and Finland are furthest along in committing to a particular disposal technology, while many others reprocess spent fuel or contract with France or Great Britain to do it, taking back the resulting plutonium and high-level waste. "An increasing backlog of plutonium from reprocessing is developing in many countries... It is doubtful that reprocessing makes economic sense in the present environment of cheap uranium."<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>In many European countries (e.g., Britain, Finland, the Netherlands, Sweden and Switzerland) the risk or dose limit for a member of the public exposed to radiation from a future high-level nuclear waste facility is considerably more stringent than that suggested by the International Commission on Radiation Protection or proposed in the United States. European limits are often more stringent than the standard suggested in 1990 by the International Commission on Radiation Protection by a factor of 20, and more stringent by a factor of ten than the standard proposed by the US Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) for <a href="/wiki/Yucca_Mountain_nuclear_waste_repository" title="Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository">Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository</a> for the first 10,000 years after closure.<sup id="cite_ref-Vandenbosch_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vandenbosch-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S. EPA's proposed standard for greater than 10,000 years is 250 times more permissive than the European limit.<sup id="cite_ref-Vandenbosch_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vandenbosch-92">[92]</a></sup> The U.S. EPA proposed a legal limit of a maximum of 3.5 <a href="/wiki/Millisievert" class="mw-redirect" title="Millisievert">millisieverts</a> (350 <a href="/wiki/Millirem" class="mw-redirect" title="Millirem">millirem</a>) each annually to local individuals after 10,000 years, which would be up to several percent of<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (March 2014)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> the exposure currently received by some populations in the highest natural background regions on Earth, though the U.S. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">DOE</a> predicted that <a href="/wiki/Yucca_Mountain_nuclear_waste_repository#Opposition" title="Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository">received dose would be much below that limit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Over a timeframe of thousands of years, after the most active short half-life radioisotopes decayed, burying U.S. nuclear waste would increase the radioactivity in the top 2000 feet of rock and soil in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (10 million km<sup>2</sup>) by <a href="/wiki/Approximation#Mathematics" title="Approximation">≈</a> 1 part in 10 million over the cumulative amount of <a href="/wiki/Natural_radioactivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural radioactivity">natural radioisotopes</a> in such a volume, but the vicinity of the site would have a far higher concentration of artificial radioisotopes underground than such an average.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mongolia">Mongolia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Mongolia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After serious opposition had arisen<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(geographic_names)" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (geographic names)"><span title="The geographic scope near this tag is ambiguous. (January 2015)">where?</span></a></i>]</sup> about plans and negotiations between <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> with Japan and the United States of America to build nuclear-waste facilities in Mongolia, Mongolia stopped all negotiations in September 2011. These negotiations had started after U.S. Deputy Secretary of Energy <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Poneman" title="Daniel Poneman">Daniel B. Poneman</a> visited Mongolia in September, 2010. Talks took place in Washington DC between officials of Japan, the United States and Mongolia in February 2011. After this the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> (UAE), which wanted to buy nuclear fuel from Mongolia, joined in the negotiations. The talks were kept secret, and although The <a href="/wiki/Mainichi_Shimbun" title="Mainichi Shimbun"><i>Mainichi Daily News</i></a> reported on them in May, Mongolia officially denied the existence of these negotiations. However, alarmed by this news, Mongolian citizens protested against the plans, and demanded the government withdraw the plans and disclose information. The Mongolian President <a href="/wiki/Tsakhiagiin_Elbegdorj" title="Tsakhiagiin Elbegdorj">Tsakhiagiin Elbegdorj</a> issued a presidential order on September 13 banning all negotiations with foreign governments or international organizations on nuclear-waste storage plans in Mongolia.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The Mongolian government has accused the newspaper of distributing false claims around the world. After the presidential order, the Mongolian president fired the individual who was supposedly involved in these conversations.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illegal_dumping">Illegal dumping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Illegal dumping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Toxic_waste_dumping_by_the_%27Ndrangheta" title="Toxic waste dumping by the 'Ndrangheta">Toxic waste dumping by the 'Ndrangheta</a></div>
<p>Authorities in Italy are investigating a <a href="/wiki/%27Ndrangheta" title="'Ndrangheta">'Ndrangheta</a> mafia clan accused of trafficking and illegally dumping nuclear waste. According to a <a href="/wiki/Whistleblower" title="Whistleblower">whistleblower</a>, a manager of the Italy's state energy research agency <a href="/wiki/ENEA_(Italy)" title="ENEA (Italy)">Enea</a> paid the clan to get rid of 600 drums of toxic and radioactive waste from Italy, Switzerland, France, Germany, and the US, with <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> as the destination, where the waste was buried after buying off local politicians. Former employees of Enea are suspected of paying the criminals to take waste off their hands in the 1980s and 1990s. Shipments to Somalia continued into the 1990s, while the 'Ndrangheta clan also blew up shiploads of waste, including radioactive hospital waste, sending them to the sea bed off the <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabrian</a> coast.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> According to the environmental group <a href="/wiki/Legambiente" title="Legambiente">Legambiente</a>, former members of the 'Ndrangheta have said that they were paid to sink ships with radioactive material for the last 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents_involving_radioactive_waste">Accidents involving radioactive waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Accidents involving radioactive waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents">Nuclear and radiation accidents</a></div>
<p>A few incidents have occurred when radioactive material was disposed of improperly, shielding during transport was defective, or when it was simply abandoned or even stolen from a waste store.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In the Soviet Union, waste stored in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Karachay" title="Lake Karachay">Lake Karachay</a> was blown over the area during a <a href="/wiki/Dust_storm" title="Dust storm">dust storm</a> after the lake had partly dried out.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Maxey_Flat" title="Maxey Flat">Maxey Flat</a>, a low-level radioactive waste facility located in <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, containment trenches covered with dirt, instead of steel or cement, collapsed under heavy rainfall into the trenches and filled with water. The water that invaded the trenches became radioactive and had to be disposed of at the Maxey Flat facility itself. In other cases of radioactive waste accidents, lakes or ponds with radioactive waste accidentally overflowed into the rivers during exceptional storms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In Italy, several radioactive waste deposits let material flow into river water, thus contaminating water for domestic use.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In France, in the summer of 2008 numerous incidents happened;<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> in one, at the Areva plant in <a href="/wiki/Tricastin_Nuclear_Power_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Tricastin Nuclear Power Center">Tricastin</a>, it was reported that during a draining operation, liquid containing untreated uranium overflowed out of a faulty tank and about 75&#160;kg of the radioactive material seeped into the ground and, from there, into two rivers nearby;<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> in another case, over 100 staff were contaminated with low doses of radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scavenging of abandoned radioactive material has been the cause of several other cases of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radiation exposure</a>, mostly in <a href="/wiki/Developing_nation" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing nation">developing nations</a>, which may have less regulation of dangerous substances (and sometimes less general education about radioactivity and its hazards) and a market for scavenged goods and scrap metal. The scavengers and those who buy the material are almost always unaware that the material is radioactive and it is selected for its <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a> or scrap value.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-104">[104]</a></sup> Irresponsibility on the part of the radioactive material's owners, usually a hospital, university or military, and the absence of regulation concerning radioactive waste, or a lack of enforcement of such regulations, have been significant factors in radiation exposures. For an example of an accident involving radioactive scrap originating from a hospital see the <a href="/wiki/Goi%C3%A2nia_accident" title="Goiânia accident">Goiânia accident</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Transportation accidents involving spent nuclear fuel from power plants are unlikely to have serious consequences due to the strength of the <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel_shipping_cask" class="mw-redirect" title="Spent nuclear fuel shipping cask">spent nuclear fuel shipping casks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 15 December 2011 top government spokesman Osamu Fujimura of the Japanese government admitted that nuclear substances were found in the waste of Japanese nuclear facilities. Although Japan did commit itself in 1977 to these inspections in the safeguard agreement with the IAEA, the reports were kept secret for the inspectors of the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Japan did start discussions with the IAEA about the large quantities of enriched uranium and plutonium that were discovered in nuclear waste cleared away by Japanese nuclear operators.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> At the press conference Fujimura said: "Based on investigations so far, most nuclear substances have been properly managed as waste, and from that perspective, there is no problem in safety management," But according to him, the matter was at that moment still being investigated.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Associated_hazard_warning_signs">Associated hazard warning signs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Associated hazard warning signs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_waste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">

<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Anderson, Mary; Woessner, William (1992). <i>Applied Groundwater Modeling</i>. San Diego, CA: Academic Press Inc. pp.&#160;325–327. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-12-059485-4" title="Special:BookSources/0-12-059485-4">0-12-059485-4</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARadioactive+waste&amp;rft.aufirst=Mary&amp;rft.aulast=Anderson&amp;rft.au=Woessner%2C+William&amp;rft.btitle=Applied+Groundwater+Modeling&amp;rft.date=1992&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-12-059485-4&amp;rft.pages=325-327&amp;rft.place=San+Diego%2C+CA&amp;rft.pub=Academic+Press+Inc.&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Plus radium (element 88). While actually a sub-actinide, it immediately precedes actinium (89) and follows a three-element gap of instability after <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a> (84) where no isotopes have half-lives of at least four years (the longest-lived isotope in the gap is <a href="/wiki/Radon-222" class="mw-redirect" title="Radon-222">radon-222</a> with a half life of less than four <i>days</i>). Radium's longest lived isotope, at 1,600 years, thus merits the element's inclusion here.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Specifically from <a href="/wiki/Thermal_neutron" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal neutron">thermal neutron</a> fission of U-235, e.g. in a typical <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Milsted, J.; Friedman, A. M.; Stevens, C. M. (1965). "The alpha half-life of berkelium-247; a new long-lived isomer of berkelium-248". <i>Nuclear Physics</i>. <b>71</b> (2): 299. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1016%2F0029-5582%2865%2990719-4">10.1016/0029-5582(65)90719-4</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARadioactive+waste&amp;rft.atitle=The+alpha+half-life+of+berkelium-247%3B+a+new+long-lived+isomer+of+berkelium-248&amp;rft.aufirst=J.&amp;rft.au=Friedman%2C+A.+M.&amp;rft.aulast=Milsted&amp;rft.au=Stevens%2C+C.+M.&amp;rft.date=1965&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2F0029-5582%2865%2990719-4&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=Nuclear+Physics&amp;rft.pages=299&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=71" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span><br />
"The isotopic analyses disclosed a species of mass 248 in constant abundance in three samples analysed over a period of about 10 months. This was ascribed to an isomer of Bk<sup>248</sup> with a half-life greater than 9&#160;y. No growth of Cf<sup>248</sup> was detected, and a lower limit for the β<sup>−</sup> half-life can be set at about 10<sup>4</sup>&#160;y. No alpha activity attributable to the new isomer has been detected; the alpha half-life is probably greater than 300&#160;y."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is the heaviest isotope with a half-life of at least four years before the "<a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Instability" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea of Instability">Sea of Instability</a>".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Excluding those "<a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">classically stable</a>" isotopes with half-lives significantly in excess of <sup>232</sup>Th; e.g., while <sup>113m</sup>Cd has a half-life of only fourteen years, that of <sup>113</sup>Cd is nearly eight <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(numbers)#1015" title="Orders of magnitude (numbers)">quadrillion</a> years.</span></li>
